Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent tactical sequence is available here. 

By moving your knight from d3 to f2 (`d3f2`), you accomplish a devastating **double check**. 
1. The knight itself attacks the White King on d1.
2. Moving the knight completely opens the d-file, unleashing a discovered check from your Queen on d7 against the White King.

In chess, the only legal response to a double check is to move the King. White cannot block both attacks, nor can they capture both attacking pieces simultaneously. This forces the White King to flee to an adjacent square (such as c1, c2, e1, or e2). 

To make matters even worse for White, the knight on f2 will also be attacking the undefended White Rook on h1 (a royal fork!). After the King scurries away to safety, you will freely capture the h1 Rook on your next turn.

An analysis of the position shows that Black is overwhelmingly ahead in material, but playing the most forcing sequence is always best to close out the game quickly and avoid any counterplay. 

Let's look at your available checks:
1. **... Qg4+** allows White's King to escape with `Kxf2` or `Ke1`.
2. **... Qd1+** also leaves `Kxf2` or `Ke1` as escape squares.
3. **... Qd3+** allows `Kxf2` or `Ke1`.
4. **... Nd3+** lets the king slip away to multiple squares (`d1`, `e1`, `d2`, `e3`, `f3`).

However, **... Qd2+** is extremely powerful. 
By placing the Queen on `d2`, it is firmly protected by your pawn on `c3`. More importantly, `Qd2` defends your Knight on `f2` horizontally along the 2nd rank. This entirely strips the White King of the `Kxf2` escape resource. The Queen also neatly slices away the `e1`, `d1`, `d3`, and `e3` squares from the King.

With `f1` occupied by White's own Bishop, White is forced into making exactly one legal move: `Kf3`. After the King is driven completely out into the open, Black will have an easy follow-up to checkmate soon (for instance, following up with `Qd1+` to chase it further to `e3`).

Pushing your d-pawn to d4 is the most principled and forcing continuation. 
- It immediately challenges White's c3 pawn.
- If White captures with `cxd4`, you recapture `cxd4`. This completely opens the c-file for your c8 Rook, pointing it right at White's Queen on c1, while severely restricting White's remaining b1 knight from developing to c3. 
- If White ignores the tension, you will confidently capture on c3, shredding their center and opening more lines toward their uncastled King.

- **g3e2, g3f1, g3h1**: These squares immediately blunder the knight to White's king or leave it trapped in the corner.

2. **Material Gain**: White's only reasonable response to avoid losing a full piece for nothing is to recapture with the d2 pawn (`dxc3`). 
3. **Follow-up**: After White plays `dxc3`, you can recapture with your dark-squared Bishop (`Bxc3`). You will have traded your Knight for a Bishop and a Pawn, resulting in a net gain of +1 pawn.

This sequence simplifies the position favorably for you while grabbing an extra pawn and improving your piece activity.

This beautifully coordinates an immediate escape while turning the tables into a lethal attack.

- Your Knight on **e5** is attacked by White's pawn on **f4**.
- Your Knight on **e4** is attacked by White's Bishop on **d3**.

If you simply retreat one of the knights, White will capture the other, resulting in a loss of material. However, you have a tactical solution that perfectly escapes this bind: your Knight on e5 can capture the very Bishop that is attacking your other knight.

After White responds by recapturing on d3 (likely with `c2xd3`), your other knight will still be attacked by the new pawn on d3, but it will be your turn again, allowing you to retreat it to safety (e.g., `e4c5` or `e4g5`). This brilliant sequence trades a knight for a bishop and saves you from losing a full piece.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

I have found a forced mate in 2. 

By playing the Queen to f2, we restrict the White King's movement severely. The Queen on f2 will be beautifully supported by our Knight on e4. 

Here is the analysis:
1. **... Qf2+** 
The White King is checked horizontally on the 2nd rank. Because our Knight on e4 protects the Queen, White cannot capture it. The White King has exactly two legal flight squares: **h1** or **h3**. 

- If White plays **2. Kh1**, we finish with **2... Ng3#**. The Knight delivers a check, perfectly covering the dark squares, and the Queen guards all the remaining escape squares (g1, h2) as well as the Knight itself. 
- If White plays **2. Kh3**, we slide the Queen over with **2... Qg3#**. Once again, the Queen directly attacks the King and perfectly covers all adjacent squares, while being fully protected by the Knight on e4. 

Since all lines lead to a forced checkmate in exactly two moves, this is the winning sequence!

make_move f1f2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent choice in this position is to advance your pawn with **c5c4**.

Here's why this move is tactically crushing:
1. **The Threat:** White's pawn on d3 is currently attacking your Bishop on e4. You need to react to this to avoid losing a piece.
2. **The Counter-Attack (Fork):** By pushing your pawn to c4, you create a beautiful pawn fork. The c4 pawn now simultaneously attacks White's Rook on b3 and the very pawn on d3 that is threatening your Bishop.
3. **The Forcing Variations:** 
- If White decides to take your Bishop (`d3xe4`), you simply capture their Rook (`c4xb3`). You've traded your Bishop for a Rook (winning the exchange), simplifying an already massively winning position.
- If White captures your pawn (`d3xc4`), the threat on your e4 Bishop is instantly eliminated! You can then comfortably recapture with your d5 pawn (`d5xc4`), renewing the attack on the b3 Rook.
- If White decides to save their Rook by retreating it (e.g., `Rb2`), you just capture the pawn (`c4xd3`), winning a clean pawn and permanently securing your Bishop. 

It completely diffuses White's only threat while forcing a material gain.

make_move c5c4

Currently, your Rook on d2 is being attacked by White's dark-squared Bishop on c1 (since they are both on the c1-h6 diagonal). We can move the Rook to safety while simultaneously creating a deadly threat. 

The most forcing and crushing move is to move the Rook down to the 1st rank:

**d2d1+**

Here is why this move is excellent:
1. **It saves the Rook:** You move your Rook completely out of the White Bishop's attack path.
2. **It forces the King:** Moving to d1 delivers a check along the 1st rank to White's King on g1.
3. **It wins the Bishop:** Once White is forced to step out of the check onto the 2nd rank (e.g., Kg2 or Kf2), you can simply capture the defenseless Bishop on the following turn (`Rxc1`), extinguishing White's last remaining active piece.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The position shows that Black is already up significant material (an extra rook and knight). Your pieces are wonderfully centralized, particularly the knight on d4, which dominates the board and serves as a powerful outpost supported by the c5 and e5 pawns. 

White's pawn structure in the center (c4, d3, e4, f3, g4) is solid but can be easily dismantled. 

By playing **b5xc4**, you force a very favorable trade:
1. If White recaptures with the d3 pawn (**dxc4**), it opens up the d-file for your d8 Rook, gets rid of White's supporting d3 pawn, and leaves White with a weak, isolated c4 pawn (since White has no a- or b-pawns left to support it).
2. If White tries to recapture with the knight (**Nxc4**), the knight abandons the defense of f3, allowing your dominant d4 knight to jump to f3 (**Nxf3**), winning a pawn and forking White's king (if it ever moves to the e-file) and the g1 Rook.
3. If White tries to recapture with the bishop (**Bxc4**), it abandons the defense of e2. You can then immediately play the crushing royal fork **Ne2+**, winning the g1 Rook.

Since White is forced into a bad structural change, this is the most principled and breaking move.

make_move b5c4

Player_Black (to Proxy):

thought
The board shows that White has only a King on c3 and pawns on e4 and h5. Black has a massive material advantage with a Rook on d8, Knights on d4 and f6, a Bishop on c4, and several pawns.
Currently, Black's Bishop on c4 is undefended. Since the White King is on c3, it is directly attacking the c4 Bishop, and White's threat is simply to capture it (Kxc4). 

Black has several ways to proceed, including checks like Nd4-b5+ or Nf6-d5+, but these checks would still leave the c4 square unprotected, allowing the White King to escape by capturing the Bishop. Given the overwhelming advantage, the simplest and most principled approach is to save the Bishop while continuing to weave a mating net around the White King.

By retreating the Bishop to d3 (c4d3), the Bishop is perfectly safe. Moreover, from d3, the Bishop defends the c4 square, preventing the White King from stepping there. Combined with the c5 pawn (which covers b4) and the d4 Knight (which covers c2 and b3), the White King becomes severely restricted, leaving it with only b2 and d2 as legal squares. This setup will lead to a very quick and inevitable checkmate in the following moves.
c4d3 is the most logical and dominating move.
```
make_move c4d3
```

Player_Black (to Proxy):

This is a completely winning endgame where you have a massive material advantage. However, you must be careful not to accidentally stalemate the opponent, as White's only other piece (the h5 pawn) is blocked by your h6 pawn. 

The most forcing and precise move is to give a check with the b5-knight:
**1... Nbc3+**

Here's why this is the best move:
- You give a safe check, forcing the White king to respond. The White king cannot step up to the second rank because your d2-rook controls a2, b2, and c2. So White is strictly forced to choose between **2. Ka1** and **2. Kc1**.
- If White plays **2. Ka1**, you have a beautiful checkmate on the next move with **2... Ra2#**. The White king will be trapped, and your c3-knight perfectly defends the rook on a2!
- By moving the *b5-knight* instead of the e4-knight, your e4-knight stays exactly where it is, importantly maintaining its defense of your d2-rook. If White chooses the other escape square, **2. Kc1**, your rook remains fully protected by your knight, avoiding any silly blunders where the king could just capture it. 

make_move b5c3

Based on the position, you have a solid King's Indian / Benoni-type structure with a powerful wedge pawn on d4. 

Let's address the most tempting move first: capturing en passant (`d4e3`). While it looks forcing, taking en passant here is actually a strategic mistake. Your pawn on d4 is a massive thorn in White's positionтАФit cramps their center, permanently locks down the backward d2 pawn, and traps the c1 Bishop. If you capture en passant, you allow White to recapture (e.g., `dxe3` or `fxe3`), which clears their cramp, activates their pieces, and opens files for their Queen and Bishop. Maintaining the d4 wedge keeps White suffocated.

Another trap to avoid is `c8g4` (Bxg4) hoping to win a pawn, but White's Bishop on h3 is perfectly aligned on the same diagonal and would simply recapture `h3xg4`. Also, moves like `c6a5` hoping to attack White's undefended b3 pawn walk straight into a pawn fork with `b3-b4`, attacking both your c5 Bishop and a5 Knight.

The best course of action is to ignore White's pawn push and focus on completing your development while improving your control of the center. 

**c8e6** is the ideal choice. It safely develops your last remaining minor piece to an active square where it is firmly protected by your f7 pawn. It eyes the c4 pawn, controls important central squares (like f5 and d5), and connects your Rooks on the back rank to complete your development.

One extremely attractive option here is advancing the d4 pawn to d3 (`d4d3`). Let's break down why this is overwhelmingly the best move:
1. **Creates a Wedge:** The pawn on d3 becomes a powerful "bone in the throat" in White's camp. It fixes White's d2 pawn permanently, which in turn completely blocks the White Bishop on c1 from developing along the c1-h6 diagonal.
2. **Restricts White's Queen:** The d3 pawn attacks the c2 and e2 squares. Because of this, White's Queen on e1 is deprived of the e2 square, leaving it severely cramped.
3. **Opens Diagonals:** Currently, the d4 pawn blocks the c5 Bishop's control over the a7-g1 diagonal. Moving the pawn to d3 opens up this bishop to directly eye the g1 Knight and the vulnerable f2 square. 
4. **Vacates a Monster Outpost:** Pushing the d-pawn vacates the d4 square. Neither of White's pawns (c4 or e4) can attack d4. This turns d4 into an unassailable, permanent outpost for Black's c6 Knight (e.g., Nc6-d4 on the next turn), which will radiate immense pressure on White's position. 
5. **Rock-solid Safety:** The d3 pawn will be immediately and safely defended by the Black Queen on the newly-opened d-file (d8-d3). 

Seeing no immediate tactical threats from White (as their pieces are largely undeveloped and pawns blocked), `d4d3` creates a long-term, winning positional advantage.

To evaluate the best move, let's take a closer look at the position and material balance. 

Right now, you are up a full Rook and a Knight! This is because your Knight on `a1` has seemingly just captured a White Rook in the corner and is now resting deep in enemy territory. White's position is very cramped, but your main priority should be consolidating this massive material advantage and making sure White gets absolutely no counterplay. 

The Knight on `a1` is currently trapped, but notice that White actually has very few pieces capable of quickly navigating to `a1` to capture it. 

The move **c5-d4** (`c5d4`) is an absolute masterclass in this position for several reasons:
1. **The Ultimate Outpost:** Placing your Bishop on the `d4` square turns it into an unassailable monster. There are no White pawns that can easily attack it (White lacks `c3` and `e3` pawns), and the `d`-file is closed down by the `d2` and `d3` pawns so White's Queen cannot pressure it.
2. **Rescuing the Trapped Knight:** By stepping onto `d4`, the Bishop immediately seizes control of the long `a1-h8` dark-square diagonal. This means your Bishop creates a laser beam straight down to `a1`, permanently *defending* your trapped Knight! If White ever tries to spend 2-3 turns maneuvering their remaining Rook or Queen to attack `a1`, they still won't be able to capture it without losing massive material.
3. **Attacking g1:** The Bishop on `d4` still maintains its diagonal pressure on White's only remaining Knight on `g1` (and limits White's king-safety options). 
4. **Prophylaxis:** Sitting on `c5`, your Bishop could eventually have been kicked by a `b3-b4` pawn push from White. Moving to `d4` sidesteps that completely.

With everything securely locked down, your material advantage easily wins the game.

Based on the board analysis, here is the thought process for the best move:

1. **Understand the immediate threat:** White has just moved a pawn or piece to `g6`, which directly attacks our `f7` and `h7` pawns. If we ignore it, White will capture one of them and severely compromise our kingside pawn shield.

2. **Evaluate the captures (`h7xg6` vs `f7xg6`):**
   - **If `h7xg6`:** We keep the `f7` pawn. The structure `f7-g6-g7` is quite solid. However, this opens up the h-file for White. Even though White's own `h3` pawn is currently in the way, White's rook is already stationed on `h2`, giving them potential long-term attacking chances if they manage to push `h4-h5`.
   - **If `f7xg6`:** We open the f-file for ourselves. Since our rook is sitting perfectly on `f8`, it will immediately gaze down the newly half-opened file. Let's look at what's on that file in White's camp: White has a pawn on `f3` and, crucially, their King is uncastled/tucked on `f1`.

3. **Tactical advantage of `f7xg6`:** 
   By opening the f-file, the `f8` Rook immediately pins White's `f3` pawn to the White King on `f1`! This absolute pin paralyzes the pawn (which was previously helping support White's center, like the `e4` pawn) and creates enormous structural pressure on White. Our King remains exceptionally well guarded behind the `g6`, `g7`, and `h7` pawns.

Capturing towards the center while actively developing a devastating pin on the opponent's king makes `f7xg6` the strongest reply.

The position shows that Black is up a massive amount of material (+13 points of material: a Rook and two Knights). White is lacking a Rook and two Knights, and White's pieces are quite passive. The Black knight on a1 is completely safe for the moment because it is shielded from the d1 Queen by the c1 Bishop, which cannot move safely without being captured by the d4 Bishop.

With such a large advantage, the goal is to play solidly, improve piece activity, and force favorable trades or win even more material without taking unnecessary risks. 

Let's look at the Knight on d7. It's on a decent square, but moving it to **b6** is very strong for a few reasons:
1. **Creates a direct threat:** `d7b6` attacks the White pawn on c4. Your light-squared Bishop on e6 is also aiming at c4. This means you will have two attackers on c4, while White only has one defender (the b3 pawn). 
2. **Restricts White's response:** If White tries to save the pawn by pushing `c4-c5`, your dark-squared Bishop on d4 is perfectly placed to just capture it (`Bxc5`), winning the pawn for free since c5 is completely undefended.
3. **Improves the Knight:** The Knight on b6 becomes an active piece, eyeing key squares like a4, c4, and d5, while completely safe from any White pawn pushes.

If White attempts to defend c4 with a passive move like Queen to c2 (`d1c2`) or King to d3 (`e2d3`), White's pieces will be tied down, giving you a free hand to develop further (e.g., `c7-c5`, `a7-a5`, or pushing the h-pawn).

Since `d7b6` carries no risk, prevents White from finding any counterplay, and sets up a free pawn capture, it is the most logical choice.

make_move d7b6

Based on the board position, you have a beautiful forced mate in two moves starting with a Queen check.

1. **Qd4+** 
By moving your Queen down the d-file to d4, you deliver a devastating diagonal check to the exposed White King on e3. 
- Your Queen is perfectly safe on d4, as it is protected by your pawn on e5.
- The White King cannot escape to f4 or d4 (both squares are guarded by the e5 pawn, and f4 is additionally covered horizontally by your Queen).
- Moving to d2 or f2 is impossible because your Knight on e4 controls both of those squares. 
- The King also cannot capture your Knight on e4 because your Queen on d4 now defends it horizontally. 

With all other escape squares blocked by White's own pawns (d3 and f3), the King is strictly forced to retreat to e2.

2. **Ke2 Qd2#**
Following the King's forced move to e2, you will follow up with **Qd2**, delivering a horizontal check that results in Checkmate. The Queen on d2 will be safely protected by your e4 Knight, and the King will have nowhere to run (the e1 and f1 escape squares are covered by the Queen and your h3 Bishop respectively).
